        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.7b10882"><meta property="og:title" content="High Rate Performing in Situ Nitrogen Enriched Spherical Carbon Particles for Li/Na-Ion Cells"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/asset/images/acsami.7b10882.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Nitrogen rich, porous spherical carbon particle with the large surface area was synthesized by simple pyrolysis of the amorphous covalent organic framework. The obtained mesoporous spherical carbon particles with dilated interlayer distance (0.377 nm), large surface area (390 m2 g–1) and high level nitrogen doping (10.9%) offer eminent electrochemical performance as an anode for both lithium ion (LIBs) and sodium ion batteries (SIBs). In LIB applications, the synthesized material delivers an average reversible capacity of 820 mAh g–1 after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g–1, superior rate capability of 410 and 305 mAh g–1 at 4.0 and 8.0 A g–1 respectively. In SIBs, the material shows the stable reversible capacity of about 238 mAh g–1 for the studied 500 cycles at 0.5 A g–1. The rate and steady state cycling performance at high current densities are impressive, being as high as 165 mAh g–1 even after 250 cycles at 2.0 A g–1."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/asset/images/acsami.7b10882.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6e9690ccc775c-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e9690ccc775c-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'f982b3cf-253d-4274-83bc-fbc4a862d2af'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e9690ccc775c-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "High Rate Performing in Situ Nitrogen Enriched Spherical Carbon Particles for Li/Na-Ion Cells", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b10882", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "High Rate Performing in Situ Nitrogen Enriched Spherical Carbon Particles for Li/Na-Ion Cells", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b10882", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "High Rate Performing in Situ Nitrogen Enriched Spherical Carbon Particles for Li/Na-Ion Cells", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b10882", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "High Rate Performing in Situ Nitrogen Enriched Spherical Carbon Particles for Li/Na-Ion Cells", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b10882", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "High Rate Performing in Situ Nitrogen Enriched Spherical Carbon Particles for Li/Na-Ion Cells", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b10882", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "High Rate Performing in Situ Nitrogen Enriched Spherical Carbon Particles for Li/Na-Ion Cells", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b10882", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "High Rate Performing in Situ Nitrogen Enriched Spherical Carbon Particles for Li/Na-Ion Cells", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b10882", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "High Rate Performing in Situ Nitrogen Enriched Spherical Carbon Particles for Li/Na-Ion Cells", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b10882", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "High Rate Performing in Situ Nitrogen Enriched Spherical Carbon Particles for Li/Na-Ion Cells", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b10882", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

E-mail: <a href="mailto:gopukumar@cecri.res.in">gopukumar@cecri.res.in</a>, <a href="mailto:smbtapsun@gmail.com">smbtapsun@gmail.com</a></div></div></div><div class="article_header-footer"><div class="article_header-footer-left"><div class="article_header-links"><input class="pub-doi" type="hidden" name="pub-doi" value="10.1021/acsami.7b10882"><a href="/doi/epdf/10.1021/acsami.7b10882?ref=article_openPDF" title="PDF" target="_blank" class="article__btn__secondary" data-id="article_header_OpenPDF"><i class="icon-open-pdf"></i><span>Open PDF</span></a><a href="#_i13" title="Supporting Information" class="article__btn__secondary internalNav" data-id="article_header_SupportingInfo"><i class="icon-supporting-info"></i><span>Supporting Information (1)</span></a><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=s0&amp;dbid=16384&amp;doi=10.1021/acsami.7b10882&amp;type=refOpenUrl&amp;url=http%3A%2F%2Fimp-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com%2Fopenurl%2F44IMP%2FICL_VU1%3Fid%3Ddoi%3A10.1021%252Facsami.7b10882%26sid%3Dliteratum%253Aachs%26pmid%3D29048872%26genre%3Darticle%26aulast%3DSelvamani%26date%3D2017%26atitle%3DHigh%2BRate%2BPerforming%2Bin%2BSitu%2BNitrogen%2BEnriched%2BSpherical%2BCarbon%2BParticles%2Bfor%2BLi%252FNa-Ion%2BCells%26jtitle%3DACS%2BApplied%2BMaterials%2B%2526%2BInterfaces%26title%3DACS%2BApplied%2BMaterials%2B%2526%2BInterfaces%26volume%3D9%26issue%3D45%26spage%3D39326%26epage%3D39335%26issn%3D1944-8244" title="OpenURL IMPERIAL COLLEGE LONDON" class="issue-item_open-url sfxLink"><img src="/userimages/9157ccea-6c85-4e5f-ac2a-6a9fd9422c17/sfxbutton-180e067c810" alt="OpenURL IMPERIAL COLLEGE LONDON"></a></div></div></div><div class="article_header-meta"><div class="article_header-meta-left"><input type="hidden" name="journalCodeForjhpLink" value="aamick"><input type="hidden" name="journalNameForjhpLink" value="ACS Applied Materials &amp; property="articleSection">research-article</div><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D39326%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DVadivel%2BSelvamani%252C%2BSivalingam%2BGopi%252C%2BVenkatachalam%2BRajagopal%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D9%26issueNum%3D45%26contentID%3Dacsami.7b10882%26title%3DHigh%2BRate%2BPerforming%2Bin%2BSitu%2BNitrogen%2BEnriched%2BSpherical%2BCarbon%2BParticles%2Bfor%2BLi%252FNa-Ion%2BCells%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D39335%26publicationDate%3DNovember%2B2017" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/asset/images/medium/am-2017-10882k_0009.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/asset/images/large/am-2017-10882k_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b10882&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Nitrogen rich, porous spherical carbon particle with the large surface area was synthesized by simple pyrolysis of the amorphous covalent organic framework. The obtained mesoporous spherical carbon particles with dilated interlayer distance (0.377 nm), large surface area (390 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) and high level nitrogen doping (10.9%) offer eminent electrochemical performance as an anode for both lithium ion (LIBs) and sodium ion batteries (SIBs). In LIB applications, the synthesized material delivers an average reversible capacity of 820 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, superior rate capability of 410 and 305 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 4.0 and 8.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> respectively. In SIBs, the material shows the stable reversible capacity of about 238 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for the studied 500 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The rate and steady state cycling performance at high current densities are impressive, being as high as 165 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> even after 250 cycles at 2.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D39326%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DVadivel%2BSelvamani%252C%2BSivalingam%2BGopi%252C%2BVenkatachalam%2BRajagopal%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D9%26issueNum%3D45%26contentID%3Dacsami.7b10882%26title%3DHigh%2BRate%2BPerforming%2Bin%2BSitu%2BNitrogen%2BEnriched%2BSpherical%2BCarbon%2BParticles%2Bfor%2BLi%252FNa-Ion%2BCells%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D39335%26publicationDate%3DNovember%2B2017" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291337&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Carbon" class="article__tags__link">Carbon</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrical%20properties" class="article__tags__link">Electrical properties</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292506&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Materials" class="article__tags__link">Materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290865&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nitrogen" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nitrogen" class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Covalent+Triazine+Frameworks&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Covalent%20triazine%20frameworks">Covalent triazine frameworks</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Spherical+Carbon&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_spherical%20carbon">spherical carbon</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nitrogen+Enriched&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_nitrogen%20enriched">nitrogen enriched</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Rate+Capability&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_rate%20capability">rate capability</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Cell&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20cell">sodium-ion cell</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">1 Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">With the rapid increase in energy demand and relentless depletion of fossil fuel reserves, the research society is catalyzed to move toward safe and reliable energy storage systems. Currently, lithium ion batteries (LIBs) are considered to be a promising technology, as it fulfils the energy requirements for all portable electronic equipment. However, the cost and depletion of lithium resources (20 ppm in Earth crust) limit its usage for electric vehicles (EVs), hybrid electric vehicles (HEVs) and other large-scale energy storage applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1, 2)</a> In recent years, sodium ion batteries (SIBs), deliberately get significant attention owing to their low cost and sustainability of sodium resources. As like Li, Na has similar chemical and electrochemical characteristic behavior and also undergoes similar kind of shuttling mechanism. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> Graphite, a well-known commercialized anode material in LIBs, can reversibly intercalate the Li-ions to form LiC<sub>6</sub> with a maximum theoretical capacity of 372 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Unlike Li, Na-ions cannot intercalate reversibly into the graphite moiety because of its large ionic radii resulting in poor mass transport, weak binding, high ionization potential and unfavorable thermodynamics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 4 to 6">(4-6)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Nongraphitizable porous carbons with the high specific surface area have received considerable attention as a high capacity anode in both LIBs and SIBs, as they afford several advantages such as easy electrolyte access, reduced ion diffusion pathway, providing more space for ion accommodation and large interface formation. Many carbonaceous materials such as hollow carbon nanowires, carbon tubes, graphene sheets, carbon spheres and carbon nanofibers, etc. have been explored as potential anodes in Li/Na ion batteries. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 7 to 22">(7-22)</a> Further, the modification of carbon host with hetero atoms as dopants substantially exhibits improved chemical and electrochemical performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23, 24)</a> Especially, nitrogen doping attracts attention because of its higher electronegativity and smaller size than that of a carbon atom. Hybridization of π electron in carbon with a lone pair of nitrogen, not only enhances the conductivity but also provides a favorable binding site for Li/Na storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 25 and 26">(25, 26)</a> The complexity involved in the substitution of nitrogen on to the carbon skeleton can be overcome by the selection of nitrogen rich precursors. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref8 ref18 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref18 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 7, 8, 18 and 27 to 44">(7, 8, 18, 27-44)</a> Further, the storage capacity of Li/Na ion mainly depends on nitrogen doping level. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> Recently, our group has demonstrated that biowastes (fish scale, garlic peel etc.) derived nitrogen doped carbon nanosheets provide a low cost anode for LIBs and SIBs, due to the great flexibility of carbon resources. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref45" aria-label="reference 24 and 45">(24, 45)</a> Further, as like graphene and rGO, the unique sheet like hard carbon helps to mitigate the severe volume expansion by acting as a buffer media for alloying and conversion type anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Covalent Triazine Framework (CTFs) is an interesting class of rigid, conjugated porous organic polymers branched/centered with triazine units. They have attracted extensively because of their rich nitrogen content and tunable properties. The amorphous CTFs are synthesized by trimerization reaction of carbonitriles to triazine rings at elevated temperatures (400–500 °C approx.) to form a high surface area porous framework. The strong covalent bonding between the lightweight elements renders the molecule with the chemical as well as thermal stability and low density. Wide synthetic diversity of this smart structural assembly endows with notable performance in various applications such as sensors, CO<sub>2</sub> capture, gas separation, adsorbent, optoelectronics, energy storage, and catalysis etc. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47 ref48 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 47 to 49">(47-49)</a> As a cathode in LIBs, the amorphous CTFs undergo unique Faradaic reaction via bipolar redox mechanism at which the triazine is in n- or p- doped state. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> Unfortunately, the poor conductivity and ionic mobility of the N-rich CTFs hammer its usage toward practical utilization for energy storage. However, thermolysis of such materials may pave the way for improved electrical conductivity and electrochemical performance. Further, as like MOF derived carbon, no additional process is required to leach the metal ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein we report, nitrogen rich spherical carbon particles (N-doped SC) synthesized form 1,4-phenylenediamine based covalent triazine framework by simple carbonization and its feasibility as an anode in Li/Na ion battery is systematically investigated. The resultant product inherits similar structural characteristics as like parent material, which consists of graphene analogue particles. The uniform grafting of nitrogen atom at the surface and edges of carbon skeleton provides ample active sites and exhibits excellent electrochemical performance in LIBs and SIBs. The remarkable performance of this carbon material can be endorsed to high level nitrogen doping, large interlayer distance and specific surface area.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">2 Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3"> Synthesis of Spherical CTF</h3><div class="NLM_p last">All the chemicals were of analytical grade purchased from Sigma-Aldrich (purity &gt;99.9%) and used without any further purification. The synthesis of CTF has already been reported in our earlier work. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> Briefly, 40 mmol of <i>o</i>-phenylenediamine was dissolved in 100 mL of 1,4-dioxane. To this solution, anhydrous K<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> was added and cooled to 15 °C. About 27 mmol of cyanuric chloride was dissolved in 50 mL of 1,4-dioxane separately and mixed at a very slow rate under inert condition. The reaction mixture was allowed to reach room temperature and refluxed for 72 h. The resulting brown precipitate was filtered and washed with 1,4-dioxane, methanol and acetone for several times before drying in vacuum oven at 120 °C. The N-rich spherical carbon particles were synthesized by direct carbonization of the as synthesized CTFs under a flow of argon gas at a temperature of 900 °C.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Powder XRD pattern of the sample was recorded with a PANalyticalX’pert PRO X-ray diffractometer using Ni-filtered Cu–Kα radiation (2θ ranges from 10–90°). The surface morphology of the material was investigated with field emission scanning electron microscope (FE-SEM, Gemini Zeiss), transmission electron microscopy (TEM, Tecnai 20 G2, FEI) and high resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM, Tecnai G2 F20 S-TWIN) with CCD imaging system. The surface chemistry of the material was investigated using X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, MULTILAB 2000) using monochromatic Mg Kα as an excitation source and C 1s (284.6 eV) as a reference binding energy. The amount of nitrogen incorporated into the carbonaceous material was quantitatively measured by employing Vario EL III – CHNS element analyzer. Fourier transform infrared spectrum was determined by using a BRUKER Optik GmbH model TENSOR 27 FT-IR spectrometer. The specific surface area was evaluated at 77 K, (AutosorbiQ, Quantachrome Instruments) using Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) calculation. The total surface area was calculated using multipoint BET based on the isotherm. The cumulative pore volume and pore size distribution were derived from the adsorption branch of the isotherm based on nonlocal density functional theory (NLDFT), assuming slit-pore geometry.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Electrochemical Evaluation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical behavior of the N-rich SC particles was examined by assembling CR-2032 type coin-type half cells, where the metallic Li/Na acts as both counter and reference electrodes. The working electrode was prepared by mixing 80 wt % of active material, 10 wt % of conductive carbon (Super P) and 10 wt % of polyvinylidine difluoride (PVdF) binder. <i>N</i>-methyl pyrrolidin-2-one (NMP) had been used as a dispersion medium to form a homogeneous slurry and tape-casted over degreased copper foil using a doctor blade. The coating was dried at 120 °C under vacuum, cut into required dimension and stored in the argon filled glovebox (MBRAUN UNILAB) where the moisture and oxygen level was maintained below 1 ppm. The electrodes were sandwiched by polypropylene separator and the active material loading was in the range of 0.6–0.7 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. For sodium ion cell, an in-house prepared electrolyte consisting of 1.0 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate (EC) propylene carbonate (PC) (1:1 by volume) along with 2% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) were used. For lithium ion cell the commercial electrolyte (1.0 M LiPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DEC) was employed. Electrochemical characterization was carried out using Autolab electrochemical workstation (GPES, PGSTAT 302N) and galvanostatic charge/discharge cycles with WonAtech (WBCS3000S) automatic charge/discharge life cycle testing system. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was obtained using Frequency Response Analyzer (Autolab PGSTAT 302N) for the coin cell with a small AC perturbation pulse of 5 mV and the frequency ranges from 100 kHz to 5 mHz. All the electrochemical studies had been done for the CR-2032 type coin cell at quasi-equilibrated conditions.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">3 Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Physical Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">The XRD pattern of the N-doped SC particle derived from covalent triazine framework is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. It is seen that the synthesized material exhibits two broad diffraction peaks which are consistent to typical graphene stacks with a low degree of graphitization. The peak observed at ∼2θ = 43.4°, corresponds to (100) crystallographic plane reflection obtained from honeycomb like sp<sup>2</sup> hybridized carbon. Similarly, peak observed at around 24.1° assigns to (002) crystallographic planes which arises from coherent and parallel stacking domains of graphene like sheets. The above discernible peaks observed form the XRD confirms that the synthesized material partially maintains the graphitic behavior after high temperature treatment. The interlayer distance was calculated for (002) plane and found to be 0.377 nm which is greater than pure graphitic carbon moiety, favoring for high Li/Na accommodation. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b depicts the typical Raman spectra obtained for N-doped SC particle. The synthesized material exhibits two predominant peaks at 1350 and 1580 cm<sup>–1</sup> assigned to disordered (D) band and graphitic (G) band of carbon materials, respectively. D band originates from the defect or breaking of sp<sup>2</sup> graphitic symmetry caused by structural disorders while the G band can be attributed to characteristic tangential vibration mode of sp<sup>2</sup> hybridized carbon moiety. The D/G band intensity is directly proportional to the degree of turbostratic disorder and the I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio is found to be 0.93, which suggests that the synthesized material has relatively the largest amount of defects such as edge modification by nitrogen, dangling bonds, sp<sup>3</sup> carbon and other topological defects. Further, this result is in consistent with the XRD analysis.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/asset/images/medium/am-2017-10882k_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/asset/images/large/am-2017-10882k_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. XRD pattern (a) and Raman spectrum (b) of N-rich SC particles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/asset/images/large/am-2017-10882k_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b10882&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As observed by FE-SEM, TEM and HR-TEM images in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a–f and their insets, the as synthesized material retains its original morphology even at high temperature treatment under an inert atmosphere. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a,b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/suppl_file/am7b10882_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information (SI) Figure S1</a>, the synthesized material is spherical in nature and made up of thin sheet like structures. The HR-TEM images prove the existence of disorderly orientated multilayered graphene like domains and the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns demonstrate typical ring features are indicating the amorphous character of the present material. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/suppl_file/am7b10882_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S2</a> describes the HR-TEM elemental mapping analysis, and it reveals that large quantity of nitrogen atoms are intrinsically incorporated into the carbon framework. Further, the homogeneous distribution of nitrogen, not only enhances the conductivity but also provides an adequate site for ion adsorption (Li/Na). The introduction of nitrogen atoms generate defect into the carbon matrix thereby, enhancing the overall electrochemical properties and the pores/voids present in the material reduces the ion diffusion path. Energy dispersive X-ray diffraction spectrum (EDS) presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/suppl_file/am7b10882_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S3</a> reveals that the amount of nitrogen present in the material is 11.35 at. %. Further, the amount of nitrogen existing the carbonized material is found to be 10.9 at. %, as confirmed by elemental analysis (by combustion), which is consistent with EDS and XPS analysis.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/asset/images/medium/am-2017-10882k_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/asset/images/large/am-2017-10882k_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) and (b) FE-SEM, (c) TEM and (d)–(f) HR-TEM images of N-rich SC particles (inset: high magnification).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/asset/images/large/am-2017-10882k_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b10882&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Nitrogen adsorption/desorption isotherms were recorded for nitrogen rich spherical carbon particles and the corresponding pore size distribution is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a and b, respectively. It can be observed that the material exhibits a typical type IV isotherm indicating the existence of meso-pores in major and micropores in the minor. The specific surface area was ∼390 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, as calculated using Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) equation and the pore volume is calculated by NLDFT method (0.43 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>). The coexistence of meso/micro pores and voids in the carbon texture favors more electrolyte access and easy diffusion. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was employed to study the elemental composition and the nature of nitrogen environment for the synthesized N-doped SC particles. As depicted in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a, the survey spectrum exhibits three major peaks which are related to C 1s, O 1s and N 1s atoms. To realize the bonding environments for all the elements, the spectra are deconvoluted using the Gaussian fitting program and are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>. The high resolution C 1s spectrum consists of four distinct peaks and the major peak observed at 284.9 eV is related to the graphitic sp<sup>2</sup> carbon revealing the arrangement of the carbon atoms in a conjugated honeycomb like structure. The peak observed at 286.2, 287.5, and 289.2 eV reflects the different bonding environment of C–N bonds related to C═N/C═O, N–sp<sup>3</sup> C bond and O–C═O respectively, which originates from the substitution of nitrogen in the carbon skeleton (as a defect). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> The deconvoluted nitrogen peak can be divided into three peaks centered at 398.4, 399.2, and 400.7 eV representing pyridinic N (N-6), pyrrolic or pyridonic N (N-5) and quaternary N (N-Q) respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42 ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref52" aria-label="reference 42 and 52">(42, 52)</a> The pyridinic nitrogen is located at the edge of graphitic carbon layer which bonds with two sp<sup>2</sup> C along with a lone pair conjugation, inducing its electron donor properties. Similarly, the five membered pyrrolic N contributes two electrons to the π system. However, graphitic nitrogen (N-Q) is positioned inside the carbon layer and bonds with three sp<sup>2</sup> carbon atom leading to generate a positive charge and electron acceptor properties. The deconvoluted N spectra show the detailed component of N-6 (25.5%), N-5 (21.4%,), and N-Q (53.1%). The existence of nitrogen, especially N-5 and N-6, provides an electrochemically active site for high lithium/sodium storage. The O 1s spectrum possesses four deconvoluted peaks at 531.8, 532.7, 533.5, and 534.3 eV corresponding to C═O, C–O, O═C–O, and C–O–H/C–O–C respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> In addition to N, the oxygen functional group also intensifies the defect in carbon structures. Further, the composition of obtained carbon material was characterized by FT-IR, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/suppl_file/am7b10882_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S4</a>. The peak obtained at 1660 cm<sup>–1</sup> is related to C═O vibration of carboxylic group. The band observed at 1380 and 1240 cm<sup>–1</sup> can be assigned to N–H bonding, derived from triazine backbone. The C═N and C≡N bonding forms of nitrogen are observed at 1600 and 2230 cm<sup>–1</sup>. The broad peak observed at 3170–3350 cm<sup>–1</sup> is related to partially overlapped N–H stretching with O–H functional groups. The above results are further manifest that the nitrogen atoms are structurally incorporated into the graphitic moiety.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/asset/images/medium/am-2017-10882k_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/asset/images/large/am-2017-10882k_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) N<sub>2</sub> isotherms and (b) pore size distribution of N-rich SC.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/asset/images/large/am-2017-10882k_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b10882&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/asset/images/medium/am-2017-10882k_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/asset/images/large/am-2017-10882k_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. XPS of survey spectrum, C 1s, O 1s and N 1s orbitals of N-rich SC.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/asset/images/large/am-2017-10882k_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b10882&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Electrochemical Performance As Anode in LIBs</h3><div class="NLM_p">Electrochemical performance and lithium ion storage capability of N-doped SC particles were investigated by employing CR-2032 coin type half-cell, with cyclic voltammetry and galvanostatic charge/discharge cycling studies, respectively. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a shows the typical cyclic voltammogram (CV) curves containing 10 consecutive cycles at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> cycled between 2.5 to 0.01 V vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li. Notably, the first cycle of CV curve is different from rest of the subsequent cycles especially, in the cathodic scan. In the first reduction process two distinct, intense peaks are observed at around 1.46 and 0.6 V corresponding to the irreversible reduction of the electrolyte with surface functional groups containing oxygen and the formation solid electrolyte interface (SEI) respectively. These two reduction peaks disappear in the subsequent cycles, indicating the formation of stable SEI. The peak observed in the region below 0.5 V is mainly attributed to the intercalation of lithium ions into the graphene analogue layers. During lithium extraction process, two broad peaks were observed at around 0.18 and 1.25 V maybe attributed to the deintercalation of lithium ions from graphene analogue moiety as well as structural defects and pores/voids present in the material. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> From the second cycle onward, all the CV curves were identical, representing the superior reversibility and cycling stability of the present material. The initial low Coulombic efficiency of formation cycle could be ascribed to the surface functional group reduction, electrolyte decomposition and SEI formation. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b and c depict galvanostatic charge/discharge cycling profiles and the steady state cycling performance of N-doped SC particle recorded for 100 cycles between 2.5 and 0.01 V at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. During the initial discharge process, the material delivered a stable reversible capacity of about 746 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> up to 26 cycles, and then it gradually increased to 820 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles. The increase in capacity with repeated cycling could be attributed to enhanced accessibility (effective activation) of unexposed micro and meso-pores during shuttling, which is a common phenomenon for porous carbon anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/suppl_file/am7b10882_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a and b</a> show the formation cycle at a current density of 0.025 A g<sup>–1</sup> and cycling profiles at different current densities of N-doped carbon particle, respectively. In the formation cycle, the material delivered a discharge/charge capacities of about 1666 and 930 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> which is almost 4.5 times higher than the theoretical capacity of graphite (372 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). The nitrogen doping and the existence of pores act as a reservoir for high lithium storage. A sloping plateau at ∼1.5 and ∼0.62 V related to the interaction between surface functional groups and lithium ions as well as irreversible SEI formation was observed in discharge profile, which is consistent with CV curves. The low Coulombic efficiency (56%), observed initially might be attributed to irreversible lithium loss and SEI formation, which is common for carbon based materials. As compared with other nitrogen doped carbon materials, the obtained Coulombic efficiency is high, which states that the present material effectively suppresses the side reactions. After the formation cycle, the Coulombic efficiency has drastically increased to above 97% for the studied 100 cycles. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d shows the steady state cycling performance of synthesized material at a current density of 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> up to 500 cycles. Remarkably, after such a long cycling, the material delivered a high reversible capacity of 623 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with above 97% Coulombic efficiency, revealing the virtuous cycling performance. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/suppl_file/am7b10882_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S6a and b</a>, as well as their inset, depicts the selected cycles of charge/discharge profile obtained at a current density of 0.1 and 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> respectively.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/asset/images/medium/am-2017-10882k_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/asset/images/large/am-2017-10882k_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Electrochemical performance of N-rich SC particles in LIBs, (a) Cyclic voltammetry curves (ten consecutive cycles) at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, (b) Galvanostatic charge/discharge cycling profile at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, (c) Capacity retention as a function of cycle number with Coulombic efficiency, (d) Steady state cycling performance at a current density of 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and (e) Rate performance at different current densities from 0.05 to 8.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/asset/images/large/am-2017-10882k_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b10882&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The rate performance was carried out to understand the electrochemical property at high current densities. The rate capability and cycling performance are important for practical utilization. To validate high rate application, the cell was subjected to rate capability test using various current densities starting from 0.1 to 8.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e. When the current density gradually increased from 0.1 to 0.25, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, and 4.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the corresponding average discharge capacities decreased from 746 to 690, 639, 565, 483, and 410 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> respectively. If the current density was further reduced to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the material restores its original discharge capacity of about 740 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. More interestingly, after a low current density cycling, the cell was again subjected to rate performance. An appreciable, average discharge capacities of 610, 540, 469, 407, and 305 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was obtained at a current density of 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 4.0, and 8.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> respectively. When the cell was cycled again at 0.75 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the same cell restored its original capacity and delivered an average discharge capacity of about 565 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for the studied 125 cycles. At all current densities, N-rich spherical carbon particle exhibited excellent cycling stability and capacity retention which is consistent with steady state cycling performance at low and high current densities. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b10882/suppl_file/am7b10882_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S7</a> shows the steady state cycling performance at high current densities for the studied 250 charge/discharge cycles. The N-rich spherical carbon particle delivered noticeable average discharge capacities of about 503, 407, and 303 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 2.0, 4.0, and 8.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> respectively. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl1" aria-label="Table 1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a> lists the comparison of the performance of N-rich spherical carbon particles with a state-of-the-art carbon derived from different precursors as an anode in LIBs.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Comparison of the Electrochemical Performance of N-Doped SC Particles with Other N-Doped Porous Carbon Materials in LIBs</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap"><table class="table" border="0"><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="left"><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="left"></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">S. no</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">material</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">carbon source</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">S<sub>BET</sub> (m<sup>2</sup>g<sup>–1</sup>)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">current density (A g<sup>–1</sup>)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">discharge capacity (mAh g<sup>–1</sup>)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">reference</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">1</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">polypyrrole nanofiber</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">poly pyrrole</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">74.5</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.86 (5C)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">302</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7');
